2~Observing astronomers are the end users of the system.
237~They range greatly in experience from occasional users of the system to very experienced users such as staff astronomers at the telescopes site and service observers.
4160~Observing astronomers will wish to concentrate on the efficient acquisition of astronomical data and on line assessment of data quality, rather than on the details of controlling the telescope and instruments.
4129~The system must offer to a user an interface which, while fulfilling the various operational requirements in the different modes and offering status information both automatically and on request at any required level, is still simple to learn and secure in its use.
58~Observing astronomers shall have no privileges as far as the direct control of the telescope is concerned.
1500~They shall not be able to send control commands directly but they must be able to enquire about the status of the telescope or any subsystem at any time.
42~The intent is not to restrict the capabilities of the observing astronomer in any way but rather to establish a single point of control and responsibility.
8~Programs, as opposed to observers, may have the capability of direct control of the telescope.
369~This would allow the observer to create an observing program which requested a telescope control function but would not allow the observer to enter a command to slew the mount.
7~Traditional interactive operation shall normally be replaced by operation via an automatic sequencer.
8~This is essential to support operational requirements such as service observing and flexible scheduling.
1~A certain degree of interaction shall be provided, meaning in this case that the user will interact with the scheduler program, rather than with the control programs directly.
20~Observing commands will normally be submitted via the User interface to a queue for later execution.
20~It must also be possible to break and resequence this queue.
12~In particular, Operations staff will be able to enable direct interactive operation, but this shall not be considered as the normal operation mode.
160~It is evident that, for some functions (such as adjustment of spectrograph slit width for seeing conditions) it must be necessary to include interactive capability.
3~However each instance of such a function should be examined as a candidate for automation such as focussing.
6~The existence of the scheduling queue shall be transparent to the on site observer during the initial phases of telescope operation.
10~Only after experience has been gained with the system will the existence of the queue become evident to the on site observer.
1~Operations staff will control the telescopes indirectly via a scheduler program or directly via commands.
48~They will supervise telescope operation and will be able to advise observing astronomers on what they have to do to use telescope and instruments efficiently.
5~They shall also monitor general performance and system safety.
21~Operations staff shall have to access all commands and maintenance procedures in case of problems.
6~They shall not have access to subsystems while these are in normal operation.
52~If they need to access other parts of the system appropriate reconfiguration procedures have to be run.
32~Operations staff shall have access to operation tables in update mode, while observing astronomers will have access to them only in read mode.
42~Again, the intent is not to restrict the capabilities of the observing astronomer in any way but rather to establish a single point of control and responsibility.
8~At the present time it is not clear how to handle visitor instrument requirements in this area.
47712~They shall be able to change the operational status of units according to the results of tests performed on such units (for example to see if a faulty unit can be declared as operational again and redefined as part of the environment in use).
3200~Software development and maintenance are staff based either at the telescopes site or base facility, or based at the telescopes remote operations facility, or non system staff from a contract company or from an associated Institute.
16~Software development and maintenance staff intervene when there is a major problem to be solved or an upgrade to be installed.
21~They also perform system generation and installation of new software packages or new releases, according to established test and validation procedures.
398~Software development and maintenance staff need the highest privilege in order to be able to modify everything in the system.
12~Nevertheless strict configuration control guidelines must be followed to prevent interference with ongoing normal operation.
32~Software development and maintenance staff usually work at the test level for the part of the software under test.
15~Other parts of the telescopes may also need to run in test mode to support integration tests.
32~they shall be able to take calibration or ﬂat ﬁeld exposures in parallel
2610~they shall be able to prepare for an exposure to start as soon as the telescope beam is switched back to them (in this case, they are in a hot standby situation)
38~they shall be able to work at all foreseen operation levels (observing, maintenance, test)
8~Regardless of the status of an inactive instrument, it shall not be possible for any of its permitted actions to adversely impact the active instrument
218~The requirement to provide for support, installation, and operation of outside instruments brought by the observer has several implications for the system.
274~Due to the specialized nature of visitor instrumentation it is unlikely that complete integration into the system environment is either feasible or warranted.
2~In this case a subset of the available functionality must be made available through a standardized interface.
84~The system Telescopes view all instruments as operating as servers, responding to commands from the upper levels of the system.
17~Visitor instruments must be capable of operating in this mode to be adequately supported.
10~It should be the goal of this interface that it be a subset of the existing instrumentation interface (rather than a separate system).
68~Due to the nature of visitor instrumentation it is unlikely that more complicated functionality can be supported.
13~In particular coordinated motions of the system components with those of the visitor instrument (other than simple raster scans) will not be supported.
6~This does not mean that more complicated functionality will not be possible for visitor instruments that require it.
60~Such functionality will not be offered as a standard service but will require a joint effort on the part of system and the visitor instrument team.
1246~As more complicated functionality will be supported via standardized interfaces for the system instruments, such as coordinated motions, it should be possible to adapt visitor instruments to this standard.
46~The difﬁculty with offering such services as an externally supported standard, as opposed to an internally supported standard, is that decisions to change internal standards do not impact external users.
1438~It is important that the visitor instrument interface be stable and long lived, as the time between successive uses of the same visitor instrument can be as long as one or two years.
2~The support of visitor instrumentation is made simpler if the visitor equipment adheres to the system standards.
224~For these reasons it is probably not possible to establish a combined standard to which all instruments, both system and visitor, adhere to completely.
8032~It is much better to establish a subset of system facilities which will be made available to visitor instruments via long lived, stable interfaces.
2~Visitor requirements outside of these would be handled on an as needed basis.
2~The support of both system and visitor instruments would beneﬁt by the provision of a system observatory simulator.
50~This simulator, appearing to the instrument as a standard set of hardware and software interfaces, would present a functional deﬁnition of the observatory
1315~The requirements to have instruments operated as single units imply that several user stations will be active at the same time on the telescopes.
60~On these user stations, different kinds of users may be working at the same time with the telescopes software.
2540~Independently of the location of users at the telescope site, they shall be able to access (according to their privileges) any part of the whole setup with a simple logon and conﬁguration operation.
204~In other words any subsection of the whole telescopes system should be accessible and controllable from any single point (but of course with protection ensuring security and safety).
2~A particularly simple case of multi use of the system is multi point monitoring.
640~By this it is meant that, while some (active) user is in control of the telescopes, someone else can follow what they are doing by monitoring all the relevant data from telescope and instruments.
3215~All other users wishing to monitor telescopes operations have to go through the procedures set up by Operations and get permission to do so.
32~The multi point monitoring mode might also be important when certain difﬁcult or rare problems occur, when expert advice is needed and can only be obtained from colleagues situated remotely.
9400~Multi point monitoring allows them to follow directly the results of tests performed and investigate how the system is working (for example by selecting different display pages with the up to date status information on different parts of the system).
38~Multi point monitoring also allows a local observer to be monitored and advised by a remote supervisor.
90~Monitoring shall exist both in the form of automatic displays of status information at different locations, and in the form of explicit access to the required status information from any point.
1~Monitoring shall not affect the performance of ongoing observations.
30~Normally executed with the sequencer by providing a computer executable program, in order to make efﬁcient use of the system telescope.
4~Interactive operation is supported, but always through the Observatory Control System (OCS).
32~There is a visual user interface to the OCS to allow for changes to the viewing program.
14~It is possible to have more than one station participate in the observing.
2~Interactive observing with time allocation for full nights is a ﬁrst basic requirement of the telescopes.
584~It is therefore an essential requirement that telescope operation is supported by the software in a smooth and very friendly way in this mode.
6~Interaction will normally be via an automatic sequencer.
990~This is clearly a top priority requirement and one which will have to be realized before implementing any other mode.
400~The initial implementation of the automatic sequencer will operate in a "pass through" mode where all commands are accepted and transmitted with minimal checking and delay.
10~The observing program must be fully automated, requiring very little human interaction during the observation.
8~The means that the system software must include a sufﬁciently rich programming environment to make this feasible.
4~In addition, this should be a visually oriented environment providing a simple, easy to use interface to the astronomer.
1704~There should be a full telescope simulator to enable the astronomer to test observing programs for completeness, errors, and functionality.
1~This simulator should function within the virtual telescope environment of the system.
9~All control software must provide support for simulated use within the virtual telescope
3440~There is a requirement for software to assist in object selection both within an observing program and across observing programs, in order to optimize observing efﬁciency.
6~This software must consider target positions, weather conditions, and instrument conﬁgurations
422748~There must be software to support the ﬂexible scheduling, both manually and via a scheduler, allowing for the interleaving of observing programs in a manner that is transparent to the individual observing programs.
120~This includes managing the collection of science, environmental, engineering, reference, and calibration data.
3980~To maximize the use of the available observing time it must be possible to queue all of the observing that is possible with the currently available instruments.
2~These would be in the form of preprogrammed observing sequences.
12~It should be possible to resort the queue so that the next observation to take place comes to the front of the queue.
1270~This sorting will be based on properties of the individual observing sequences, current site conditions, and other rules established by the observatory directorate.
3~Thus queue scheduling is a superset of preprogrammed observing, with similar requirements.
78~As the costs of implementing such a scheduler are currently difﬁcult to estimate it may prove necessary to implement it in a future phase of the project.
12~The system design, if it does not include a scheduler, should speciﬁcally allow for its future implementation.
5~All software should be developed to permit remote operations.
2~There should be no conceptual difference between software working on site and remotely.
8~All observing facilities should work both on site and off site.
6~It should be possible to do full operations remotely
4~Team observing, with multiple observers at different sites should be supported.
18~It must be possible to restrict speciﬁc operations to speciﬁc remote sites.
2~The method used to restrict such operations should be independent of the operations themselves, and dynamic.
105~We will take advantage of commercially available protocols such as ISDN, TCP or IP, Internet, etc.
18~The choice of the name remote operations is meant to suggest an entire category of operations, such as remote observing but also remote access for diagnostic support.
24~Despite the obvious limitations introduced by the link bandwidths available at the different locations, the system shall be totally transparent to local or remote use.
104~It is only necessary that the functionality of the system be transparent, it is accepted that the speed of the link will determine the perceived transparency of the system.
3~The system design should minimize the impact of link bandwidth on transparency.
6~Security of operation shall be considered and might imply different operation levels and privileges at different sites.
2108~It is required that the remote operations software be considered from the beginning in the telescopes software design, to avoid redesign later.
72~This should reduce the amount of speciﬁc software needed for remote operations, as the common layers of software shall cope from the beginning with a distributed environment.
114~Remote control means that the function normally associated with a local Telescope Operator, that of entering telescope control commands, would be available from a remote site.
4~In practice remote control will be restricted to the telescopes Enclosure and telescopes Control Facility.
2~Remote monitoring is the simplest level of remote observing.
268~It is a requirement for the telescopes operation and will complement service observing, making it friendlier for users.
135~Remote monitoring coincides to a large extent with multipoint monitoring, but allows the remote user to "pick and choose" the information that is displayed on the remote screen.
4~There is no requirement that the remote screen be a duplicate of the local screen.
4~The remote keyboard will have no effect on the local user's environment.
18217~A remote observer might also need a real time video and voice link with the operator in the control room, perhaps using a portable video camera which the operator can position as necessary.
1~Quite apart from remote observing, remote access to the telescopes and its instruments is required for monitoring and diagnostic purposes.
48~This might be necessary to back up local users expertise and to help in case of problems.
2~Remote access, in this case, must be possible from the telescopes base facility.
4~Distributed access to the telescopes software, once implemented.
432~also allows, without extra requirements, local access (at the telescopes site) or remote access (telescopes support, base, and remote operations facilities).
10~The observing program must be automated, requiring little human interaction during the observation.
8~The means that the system software must include a sufﬁciently rich programming environment to make this feasible.
4~In addition, this should be a visually oriented environment providing a simple, easy to use interface to the astronomer.
20~This programming environment should be available both to the astronomer, for developing the program, and to the observer, for review and adjustment of the program.
2~This access may or may not be done concurrently on a shared environment.
5688~The programming environment should allow for the communication of special notes, instructions, and comments from the astronomer to the observer, possibly involving multimedia techniques.
1758~To achieve efﬁcient service observing, queue observing, and ﬂexible scheduling, it should be possible to carry out observations automatically, in accordance with predeﬁned sequences of exposures, as is commonly done in space observatories.
2~This corresponds to what is meant by planned observing, which still requires competent monitoring at the telescopes site or remotely.
132~At the same time, one does not want to lose the advantages and the extra ﬂexibility of ground based astronomy.
16~So whichever scheme is adopted to perform automatic sequences, interaction shall be allowed at the desired level.
108~The syntax of control ﬂow commands is to be consistent across the system, whether accessing workstation software or IOC software.
25~All subsystems must respond to a common set of commands to test operational status, inquiries as to version, perform self tests, etc.
86~All IOC subsystems must respond to additional common commands for such activities as start, stop, initialize, reset parameters, etc.
257~The support structure for communicating commands must be reliable, with a uniform ACK or NAK protocol adopted across all systems.
5~Timeouts must be supported at approximately 500 msec.
8~Handshaking of commands between IOCs must occur within 100 200 msec, signaling acceptance of each command.
3~For commands allowing delayed replies, timeouts for that reply must also be supported.
4~Peak control information within the system is expected to be 100 TPS, this assumes bridging between communication sections, to isolate trafﬁc in relevant sections only.
32~For focusing and related activities, maximum acceptable detector readout time is about 0.1 sec, though only a portion of the detector may be read during that time.
2~For mosaicked, large optical detectors, a full readout of the detector must be done in about 2 or 3 minutes.
264~Since the system supports monitoring of operation, there must be the capability of providing multiple, simultaneous access to data.
36~Data transfer between the virtual telescope system and attached workstations therefore imposes signiﬁcant transfer requirements on the LAN.
8~The LAN must support a transfer rate of 20 40 Mbits or second.
24~Data is normally acquired as uncompressed data, but may be compressed using a loss less compression technique for transmission from the system or across the system LAN.
160~The goal of compression is to minimize bandwidth impact on the LAN and WAN and to save space on removable media.
24~For data that requires preprocessing, such as infrared detector data, only the preprocessed data is stored.
16~Data from all instruments and detectors is stored as compressed data, using a standard format.
108~There is a ﬁrst level of storage within IOCs, to secure data in the event of link failures.
240~A second level of storage is on the system data disk(s), possibly also on removable media.
4~Quick look data quality assessment is done using this level.
16~Archiving of data is automatically done while in observing and maintenance level operation to the system Archive subsystem.
12~Shipping of data to a central archive follows later.
10~Data is transmitted between system and home Institutes using a FITS format and contains all header information provided with the data.
32~The data capacity of the system is limited by transfer methods and technology, as well as archiving capacity on site.
3552~The system data capacity is capable of retaining 7 days of data produced by the largest instrument, the last 3 days of which must be available interactively from hard disk or similar medium.
4~The system must allow for fast transmission of rough images every 0.5 sec.
80~This may be assisted through the use of data loss compression techniques (for example JPEG, MPEG, etc).
14~In addition, there is the need for transmission of images matching the original resolution.
12~This high quality transmission must require less than 20 sec, and can only be assisted with loss less compression.
1399~To preserve the integrity of the system, there must be a system of privileges established at each operating level of the system.
20~These privileges should be determined in a simple manner during logging into the system.
160~Protection against accidental interference is to be implemented using an Access Mode Allocation system that dynamically identiﬁes and assigns resources as needed.
2~Critical resources (those that can support only a restricted number of simultaneous uses) are assigned solely through this allocation system.
5~The allocation system must ensure that the system cannot remain deadlocked with respect to this resource allocation.
2~Finally, procedures must be implemented for convenience and system integrity, to simplify and codify common tasks.
11~The telescope and instrument software shall contain built in test (BIT) facilities to verify telescopes system and telescopes software performances.
40~Every telescopes software module shall have corresponding test speciﬁcations to check normal operation of releases, to be used both for acceptance tests and as an on line test procedure.
9~The telescopes control software shall also provide for execution of self test sequences of the telescopes system and subsystems.
5~These shall automatically exercise all subsystems present in a given operational conﬁguration.
1~Regression tests should be a part of every telescopes software package
1~Subsystems must notify the user when faults occur.
5~This notiﬁcation must be speciﬁc as to origin and problem.
2~The notiﬁcation must also be capable of being electronically logged.
1216~It may also prove useful to have multiple levels of fault notiﬁcation such as detailed, verbose, short, to aid in tracking down problems.
916~Should a subsystem fail (for example one detector, one instrument) predeﬁned procedures must exist to redeﬁne the environment in such a way that operation can restart with the remaining equipment.
896~In case of computer hardware failure concerning the user station equipment, it shall be possible to transfer control from one user station to another via a simple software reconﬁguration procedure.
40~In the case of IOC failure, no transfer of control to another IOC will be possible, due to the local connections and interfaces to the control electronics.
12~In this case there shall be a procedure to replace faulty cards and or or assemblies.
3009984~If it is possible to observe with that particular IOC in a failed state (in general, this is limited to IOCs that are associated with individual scientiﬁc instruments) then it must be possible to reconﬁgure the system to do so.
44~Full redundancy is not a requirement of the telescopes and it will be acceptable to have to replace units in case of failure.
92~There are subsystems which are relatively inexpensive to support as redundant systems, such as telescope control computers.
764~For each area where redundancy is decided to be cost effective, procedures for switching to the backup system will be established.
10~There is no requirement for automatic switching to the backup system.
36~All communication shall be based on the use of standard communication protocols, where retry procedures are applied (a form of software redundancy) as part of the protocol.
1908~Certain network concepts may be preferable as they offer intrinsic redundancy (for example double loops) and re routing possibilities in case of node failures (single point failure protection).
18~There should be no restrictions imposed by the software on users.
192~Only policy decisions (permissions, access privileges, etc.) should prevent any user from accessing any part of the system from any local or remote station.
3~Similar functionality should be presented to the users using similar user interfaces.
7~User interfaces should clearly reﬂect access modes and operating levels.
128~Commercial packages, off the shelf public domain software, and standards are to be used whenever feasible.
6~Existing external software will be integrated with the system software.
2~The interfaces involved in this integration are considered part of the system software system.
69~All system software is to be developed using standard methodologies and development environments.
35~One of the goals of system software is that all components be easily (preferably automatically) combined into an integrated system.
283~system software developers should maintain accurate change logs showing software modiﬁcations as they are applied to the system software.
9065~system software developers should adhere to a standard method for the reporting and recording of errors from both internal and external sources.
2~system software should be developed in evolutionary fashion, using the CVS version control system
16~All system subsystem packages should include as part of the software both a simulator module for inclusion in the virtual telescope, and user interface modules for the user interface environments that the subsystem will be operating in.
12~All system software is to be fully documented, internally with appropriate comments, and external documentation.
1~External documentation must include Unix style man pages.
44~All system subsystem packages must provide modules for the testing and diagnosis of the subsystem.
2~All instrumentation control software must provide full access to all instrument functionality.
24~It is likely that different user interface modules would present different portions of this functionality to the user.
4~The information required of each interface module is found in the Functional Requirements speciﬁcations for each instrument.
4~All system software must be version labeled, both in source and binary form.
60~The version information is to be retrievable from executing software via control commands.
18~There are different requirements for software running on different layers.
1~Strict real time control is restricted to the IOC layer.
60~No subsystem package should make any assumptions about the surrounding environment beyond that provided in the interface speciﬁcations.
18~The ﬁnal purpose of the telescopes software is the acquisition of astronomical data in digital form in the most efﬁcient way.
35880~To achieve this, many other data concerning the telescope and instruments (parameters) and control commands will have to be exchanged between different processing units in order to setup and control telescope and instruments.
90~Control information must be transferred, typically in the form of commands and replies from users, to telescope and instruments.
1380~Replies might contain status information and, in general, data concerning instruments and telescopes, to be stored together with the astronomical data.
88~Control information on all controlled variables must be provided by all subsystems on request.
9~No request for information shall produce a delay of control activities or locking, even if the corresponding equipment is not available or faulty.
2550~Delay times for the exchange of control information must stay within precise time limits to be deﬁned in "General Description" in Chapter 2.
26208~One can afford to retransmit commands in case of transmission error or collision, but the protocol has to be predictable in that commands cannot get lost and replies have to come back reliably.
4~In a number of cases, synchronization with the Time Reference System at the telescopes site is also necessary.
99324~Access to control parameters, telescope and instrument information for monitoring or other use makes a signiﬁcant contribution to the control ﬂow, and may be logged at quite high rates for short periods (that is up to 200 Hz for some information).
72784~It is explicitly required that all such information is available to the telescopes software and is capable of being available to all users of the telescopes, subject only to restrictions with respect to updating.
10~It must also be possible to restrict user access to such information.
3~In particular also, the meteorological information coming from a weather station should be available centrally.
19680~Detector data must be acquired and stored in the most effective way technology will allow effectiveness should be evaluated in terms of cost, space requirements, longevity, and speed.
3~This shall lead to the deﬁnition of a telescopes standard, used on all instruments.
2~In general, operational overheads must be kept as low as possible, to maximize actual observing times.
36~Intermediate storage of raw data in memory on different nodes and in different formats should be kept to a minimum.
1344~There must be at least two copies one to secure data as acquired and one to do assessment of data quality on line (this last copy preferably on removable media).
64~The link chosen to transfer data should represent as small a bottleneck as possible for data acquisition.
16~Normally, raw data will be acquired and stored as such for quick look evaluations.
64~There might be cases where fast preprocessing is needed and where raw data will not be stored as such, but in a preprocessed format.
11280~Astronomical data will have to be transported between system and the home institutes of visiting astronomers in FITS format (as deﬁned by NOST 100 1.0, "Deﬁnition of the Flexible Image Transport System (FITS)", NASA Science Ofﬁce of Standards and Technology).
76~TV data concerning site monitoring and voice need to be capable of being available at all operations facilities.
16~It will be a question of interfacing and bandwidth costs whether such information is actually available at a speciﬁc location.
12~It is not a requirement that point to point video be available between telescopes operations facilities.
61~It is a requirement that voice connectivity, perhaps point to point, be available on a permanent connection.
2668~Other astronomical information such as that coming from sky ﬁeld monitors, autoguider cameras and sky monitoring devices such as cloud and seeing monitors shall also be capable of being available.
5491918~The capacity of the system can be expressed in terms of nodes, which is deﬁned as the number of workstations, or in terms of users, which is deﬁned as the sum total of users at all the nodes.
13~Each node will have the capability to run at all operation levels.
72~When the telescopes telescope is used in its normal observing mode, there will be a single operator node for the telescope and two data acquisition and instrument control nodes.
640~Some tests might be run in parallel on instruments that do not have the light beam at that moment, so in principle additional nodes might be working at the same time.
48~The system will provide for one auxiliary data acquisition and instrument control nodes.
1~In addition, the system must support off site observing modes.
156~The system will provide for a single off site data acquisition and instrument control node to be located at either the telescopes Site Support or Base Facility.
33~One supervisor will monitor the system, and other users might need to monitor the running of observing programs, locally or remotely.
8~The system will provide for a single local monitoring node and a single remote monitoring node.
4746~The telescopes control software shall allow simultaneous operation of up to six active control nodes and up to two more monitoring nodes (one local and one remote) without appreciable degradation of performance.
1575504~In practice the operation and facilities foreseen so far for the telescopes will limit this number to a maximum in the order of three active nodes, but the telescopes computers and software shall be capable of coping with the load of 10 active nodes, should the case arise
51~Every command must be acknowledged in a positive or negative way before the occurrence of the corresponding action within given response times.
36~There must be automatic procedures to implement startup and shutdown of the telescope and instruments.
1~These must allow startup and shutdown of instruments independently of the telescope and without affecting the telescope operation.
1~Reconﬁguration procedures must exist, to change the observing environment.
24~The deﬁnition of the observing environments must be dynamic, that is feasible during operations without the need to restart everything.
1856~Operations staff have privileges to change the environment, meaning selecting a suitable combination of instrumentsThe operational software should know which subsystems are installed and operational at any given time.
4~The user interface deﬁnes the way users see the telescopes system.
56~Given the large number of instruments, there can be many different stations which are active at the same time.
1232~It is essential for operational and maintenance reasons that, in spite of the obvious differences of the setups and commands available, the same philosophy is applied throughout.
348~This calls for a homogeneous user interface, which can be achieved only by applying the same user interface tools to the whole project, providing the telescopes user interface's ‘look and feel'.
3~The user interface should not be seen as a package linked to a speciﬁc computer.
4212~Given the requirement to be able to access the telescopes from several points, the user interface should rather be seen as a package to be callable from a large number of stations, depending on where a user is.
48~It should also be network transparent so that it does not matter where it is being run.
1~The user interface tools shall be based on standards, which will be portable across different computer hardware platforms.
92~The intent of a portability requirement is to facilitate migrating existing and future system systems to different hardware as the need arises.
9781~It is the current intent to limit the selection of computer hardware platforms to as few as is practical
6119~The telescopes software covers all aspects of control and data acquisition related to the telescope, instruments, and auxiliary instrumentation.
12~It also covers all the operational aspects of the telescopes, including on line scheduling and rescheduling.
32~There is also software which, although it will be interfaced to the telescopes, is referred to as external.
2~The telescopes software must interface to the external software and clearly the interfaces are fully part of the telescopes software.
640~Quick look data processing should be provided on the telescopes, with procedures suitable for fast on line data preprocessing.
3656~A prerequisite for this is that acquired data are made available as directly as possible in a common format, and that all additional data related to an exposure and logging information are made available on line at the same time.
272~Quick look should be usable within exposure sequences to provide results and feedback parameters to the control software in a programmed way, without the need for manual intervention.
20~This document does not try to be speciﬁc about the requirements for Quick look other than that it should be synchronous.
732~Near line processing should be available for simple data reductions required for data integrity validation (that is remove instrument and observatory effects so the observer can make decisions about further observing actions).
8~This data reduction proceeds sequentially through requests, but asynchronously from data acquistion.
44~In particular, data acquisition takes precedence over near line data reduction.
16~Off line pixel processing for full data reduction should also exist at the telescopes site, but does not have any interface to the telescopes software.
64~The Astronomical communities have made considerable investments in image processing software, and therefore, compatibility with and adaptations to these packages must be sought.
904~It should also be noted that some telescopes subsystems, such as adaptive optics, may require their own special on line pixel processing software, which is better deﬁned in the requirements for those subsystems.
106380~This is largely due to the difﬁculty of applying on line the same algorithms used for full off line reductions, in general due to the time critical nature of the image processing needs.
52~The same situation might also occur with other instruments, where speciﬁc observer support software has to be foreseen for on line use.
4536~In all these cases the speciﬁc on line (quick look) software development shall be seen as a subset of the development for the off line data reduction system, to avoid as far as possible duplication of development effort.
8~The output format of the telescopes data must be compatible with the system archive requirements.
80~As comparisons with previous data might be of great value and affect the actual observing program, on line interactive access to the data archiving system should exist, so that access to this database is possible for telescopes users.
42~The speciﬁc types of data available ﬂat ﬁelds, calibrations, science exposures, etc.
100~the amount of a speciﬁc exposure available header only, averaged exposure, complete raw data set and the time frame within which such data will be made available same night, weekly, after proprietary period will be established by the system Archiving Requirements.
2~Computer access to star catalogues is also required, so that an automatic selection of candidate guide and standard stars can be made.
6~The telescopes software must be able to interface with all commercial software packages available on the telescopes and integrated into the telescopes operation.
1184~A relevant example of such a package is a general database management system (DBMS), where operational information such as schedules, logs, problem reports and maintenance information related to various pieces of equipment should be kept.
16~The LAN shall support the majority of the telescopes system internal communication needs.
8504~This LAN must be capable of dealing both with the data bandwidths required (at peak and on average) and with the required response times and synchronization needs.
3780~This LAN shall be supplemented with a Local Time Bus, for distribution of absolute and relative time signals, and both a digital reﬂective memory bus and an analog event based bus, for distribution of signals with requirements not satisﬁed by a LAN.
2~Peer to peer connectivity should only be used to overcome a demonstrated performance problem.
80~Bypassing the hierarchy (connected between grandmother and granddaughter with no path through the mother) should only be used for transmission of status information or bulk data, not control ﬂow
160~It is envisaged that observing astronomers who have travelled to the telescopes site will make use of the telescopes control room facilities.
1104~This will allow centralized support and coordination of their operations, providing both operations support for individual instruments and supervision for all of them.
25~To allow coordination both locally at the telescopes site between the various users and with remote users, the software shall support access to the system from any user station.
2092~It will then be an operational decision, implying privileges and priorities for the various categories of users, and deﬁnition of what a given user can actually do.
2924~Access from any user station will make user stations in principle identical and software conﬁgurable as the user station of this or that subsystem.
5~This should greatly simplify the coordination problem posed by the large number of simultaneous users.
8~Individual instruments must be able to run fully independently.
272~Telescope software at the two telescopes must be maintained to be identical in the upper layers (even if hardware should differ).
9~Additions of new instruments should aim, as a goal, at introducing no modiﬁcation to already operational parts.
2~Modiﬁcations should be conﬁned to the operational procedures and should not affect the bulk of the existing software.
4~Switching to different conﬁgurations must be possible at any time with appropriate procedures.
6~There must be easy procedures to reconﬁgure the system when subsystems are modiﬁed or removed.
32~The number of main packages of software must be kept to a minimum to facilitate maintenance, but compatibly with the need to have the right degree of modularity.
4~Commercial and public domain packages should be used whenever possible.
9~Existing software packages should be reused wherever possible.
7~Existing software expertise should be consulted whenever possible.
1~All software which does not directly control speciﬁc hardware must be written as machine independent, portable code.
112~Even for microprocessor software, the software should be hardware independent, to allow a later choice of the target microprocessors.
3~To allow for expansion and maintenance, telescopes standards must be deﬁned for the on line software and the development environment.
1~On line version control must be implemented.
16~That is, the version control system must be available to recover or restore versions at all times.
2~At boot time, the telescopes software shall check the consistency of versions of all the various software components.
2~Table driven software should be used whenever possible, to avoid unnecessary compilations.
3546~Whether the software is table driven, message driven, or a combination of both is a function of the individual work packages and deﬁned in the appropriate work package description.
148~Changing system constants, such as arcseconds or bit for an encoder, shall not require recompiling but will be updated as part of system startup, and, for some constants, will be modiﬁable during operation.
14~System status parameters will be maintained to an extent that will allow restarting the system and regaining the previous state.
4~The extent of duplication of the previous state will be dictated by safety and practical considerations.
4~Strict checking should be applied on this to preserve maintainability and reconﬁguration of the system.
27952~The GSR sets as a requirement 2% and a goal 1% for total system downtime due to failures, this translates to a maximum of 15 minutes per night or 1 night per month of downtime.
22~This in turn sets quite stringent requirements on both MTBF and MTTR for the software and controls.
6064~To guarantee maximum availability of the control system, retry procedures must be embodied in the software in case of error or failure to achieve recovery on line whenever possible.
64~Should recovery also fail, the error or failure has to be reported in a clear form (to identify the cause of the problem) and the system shall put itself into a safe state, whenever a safety aspect might be involved.
16000~To avoid unnecessary downtime, it must be possible for the system to reconﬁgure itself in order to continue observing, in a different mode if required, given the failure of a single non critical subsystem.
257~To increase software robustness, range checking and validity checking shall be supported before execution of any input command.
243~This must be possible ahead of time, preparing observing sequences for automatic observations and simulating observations to estimate results.
1260~On line pre checking of the operational status of equipment should be done prior to sending critical or time consuming commands.
8814~It must be possible to apply continuous monitoring to all subsystems on request, both when in operation and when idle, to check their operational status.
6~A measure of fault rates should be done during commissioning to establish baseline rates for system reliability monitoring.
266~There are to be recovery procedures to restart after error failure.
397~The system should be constantly monitoring active subsystems to be sure they are operating correctly before sending command to each subsystem.
1~This monitoring should continue on inactive subsystems.
325~The goal for recover and or or reconﬁguration is 5 minutes from onset of the error condition to observing again.
1~Maintenance requirements including an estimate of required resources.
1~The method of upgrading the system to add capabilities and performance.
12~Areas where upgrades are anticipated should be identiﬁed with an estimate of the required effort and resources.
4500~Each subsystem should have a background task running whenever that subsystem is operational, performing such tasks as checking power supply levels, temperatures, performance, correct responses to commands.
18~Each subsystem should provide a module for fully exercising all subsystem components, both hardware and software.
4~This module is executed automatically during start up and on demand through the deﬁned interface.
8~Problems are to be automatically reported to the OCS via the deﬁned interface.
12~There are also software modules for testing the subsystem as an integrated portion of the entire system.
27~This software would be executed on demand during maintenance operation level.
12~Quantitative maintenance requirements will be allocated to the system, subsystems, and each component.
5~The requirements must be achievable and stated in such a way that veriﬁcation is permitted.
10~All equipment shall support a programmed adjustment and maintenance interval of 30 days or longer
15574~The system Control System development effort will obey and abide by both the letter and the spirit of all applicable engineering practices, laws, regulations, and policies.
7~All necessary safety approvals will be obtained before devices will be accepted.
93~The system must be self monitoring to invoke safety monitoring to prevent risk to people or damage to equipment.
46~The software should be able to quickly bring the system to a safe state upon notiﬁcation of such danger.
9~Subsystems must be able to detect such danger and report it appropriately.
3892~In the event that the risk persists, subsystems must be able to move themselves into safe states to protect people and equipment (that is if there is a failure in the higher level systems).
558950~Safety protection must be applied whenever there is the risk that the actions of the control software could endanger people or cause damage to any telescopes subsystem, for example, by driving beyond limits or by overexposing detectors.
1~This protection, where implemented, must be independent of the software.
8~In general this will require mechanical hard stops, electrical interlocks, electrical hard limit switches, soft limit switches, software limits, and watch dogs.
1044~All hazards capable of causing death and or or loss of irreplacable equipment shall be passively interlocked.
2594~All hazards capable of causing injury and or or severe damage to equipment shall be actively interlocked, severe damage implies that repairs are not repairable at the depot level
2979600~Since the system software will be developed in stages over a period of years, and since computer technology is expected to evolve rapidly over this same period, the software is to be designed to be easily extended and upgraded with modiﬁcations to non changing components.
40~The software itself, its installation process, and its documentation must be developed with this expandability in mind, using general industry standards.
34~All software is to be developed using typical modularization and standardization techniques.
2~Each module's environment is strictly deﬁned by its interface to other components.
3~No module can rely upon information outside of this interface.
4~Module selection should be done in logical fashion to minimize the size of the interfaces between modules.
1~The on line databases can be considered part of this interface, but are only accessible through their deﬁned interfaces.
5516~The software must be strictly modular, that is the functionality of a subsystem should correspond to that which belongs to that subsystem and only to that, so that software for different subsystems can be installed and maintained independently of all the rest.
34~This is needed in particular for multi instrument operation, for example, as instruments share the same subsystems on the telescope.
8~At the same time, the possibility must exist to acquire information about other parts of the system.
3~It also important that there are no undesired interactions between subsystems.
8~This may be enforced either at the client or server interface or at the message system level.
588~The security and safety of the system should be guaranteed even in the event of failure of any component, including the higher level software.
4~The ability to reconﬁgure the software if one actuator fails is desirable.
48~Data redundancy is also a requirement, to prevent a single failure from causing the loss of collected data.
6~The goal is to minimize the effects of single point errors throughout the system.
36~As much as possible, the system is to take advantage of parallel operation to improve efﬁciency.
11138~The Telescope Control System should be capable of detecting and invoking parallel operation as it is responsible for control of all of the telescope and enclosure subsystems.
65708~The development environment for the telescopes software consists of the computer hardware and system software (operating system, languages and tools) chosen optimally to support the development model presented in the previous section on life cycle aspects.
9116~Test procedure methods have to be deﬁned in the Software Test Plan (STP), while test plans shall be written for all individual software packages and modules comprising the telescopes software.
56~Apart from the component and integration test procedures, a formal release system should exist at package and module level, which should be checkable on line by the operational procedures for consistency.
10~Every system must thus be able to supply its current version upon request.
9~This section presents the speciﬁc attributes and requirements for system software.
4~Only the high level requirements for system software are presented here.
4~Detailed speciﬁcations for the subsystems are found in the individual chapters of the Software Design Description.
10~The system maintenance philosophy is described in the Software Management Plan (SMP).
1~Preventative maintenance is scheduled as speciﬁed in the system Design Requirements Speciﬁcation.
34~All software is to be developed using typical modularization and standardization techniques.
2~In particular, each module's environment is strictly deﬁned by its interface to other components.
3~No module can rely upon information outside of this interface.
4~Module selection should be done in logical fashion to minimize the size of the interfaces between modules.
504~The on line databases can be considered part of this interface, but are only accessible Reliability and availability A measure of fault rates should be done during commissioning to establish baseline rates for system reliability monitoring.
266~There are to be recovery procedures to restart after error failure.
416~During science planning, there should be validity and feasibility checks to help ensure effective and efﬁcient use of the telescope.
1~Where appropriate, these checks should also be performed during operation.
1216~The system should be constantly monitoring active subsystems to be sure they are operating correctly before sending commands to each subsystem.
1~This monitoring should continue on inactive subsystems.
8~All telescope, instrument, and detector control information is to be available at any operation level.
157~Access times to the database are to be in the range of 2 3 msec per access.
2~Asynchronous writes are to be supported, allowing for concurrent operation.
7~Time access critical information is available in memory.
47~There is to be a consistent and logical (that is name based) access method.
6~The database must support both remote access and distributed data.
3~A fundamental criteria of system telescope operation is that it support a full implementation of remote operations.
10469~This includes remote observing, remote control of telescope, enclosure, and instruments, multipoint monitoring, remote monitoring, remote access for testing, development, diagnostics, and maintenance.
1288~It is expected that all operational capability found in on site operations is extended to remote operations, with some degradation in performance resulting from WAN bandwidth considerations.
130~This means the video data signals must be encoded digitally and transferred via the WAN to remote sites.
384~There must be some form of security to control access to system features, possibly restricting some operations to speciﬁc remote sites.
11~The observation is performed through a preplanned program requiring little or no interaction with the observer.
5~Science planning and program changes are accomplished through interactive operation.
48~Is is possible to enter interactive operation from automatic operation to handle exceptional conditions.
1026~Overall performance of the system telescope is deﬁned as the percentage of viewable time during which exposures have been taken (that is sum of all exposure times over available time for exposures).
2~To improve this performance, all possible concurrencies in system operation should be used.
2~Best use of concurrency occurs when using the Sequencer.
28~Cold start up, starting the system from scratch (including time to download all software) should take about 5 minutes.
19~This does not include time to start up the telescope or instruments.
80~Warm start up, starting from scratch but also excluding software download time should take about 1 minute.
8~Telescope start up, measured from end of cold or warm start up should be about 4 minutes.
2~Instrument start up, measured from end of telescope start up, should take 2 minutes or less.
60~There must be a way to shut down all subsystems (hardware and software).
266~All start ups and shut downs are to be automatically logged with time stamps, to allow for statistics on system availability.
1696~It must be possible to log engineering data at up to 200 Hz rates for short periods of time.
2~This data must be available to external software packages for analysis.
250888~Long term logging of engineering data must be possible at slower (1 Hz or less) rates, into a common format (baselined as SYBASE).
7~Fatal errors occur if there is no acceptable recovery procedure that will allow operation to proceed.
8~Under fatal error conditions, the system falls back to a safe "backup" state requiring human intervention for restart.
13~Under serious errors, the system does not need to move off line, but the current operation cannot be completed.
4~Serious errors require human intervention to restart full operation.
2~All other unexpected conditions result in warnings that are properly logged.
732~In addition to start up procedures, there must be well deﬁned recovery procedures for any subsystem that has become inoperative.
20~Command retries must be included in the system for most common timeouts or noresponse conditions.
3~These retries should occur automatically in the command handling to avoid unnecessary error conditions.
18~Normally, there is no recovery possible from a fatal error except to shut down and then restart the subsystem.
304~In the case of an instrument failure, it may be possible to continue operation by rescheduling to use observations that do not require that particular instrument
10~For serious errors, it may be possible to continue operation with degraded performance.
26~Failure of automatic tracking may require manual tracking other errors may result in operation with a different instrument
1~Under normal conditions, the number of warnings should be small.
335~The system should monitor the rate of warning messages since an increase might indicate that some tuning or maintenance is appropriate.
7~Ideally, such conditions should be noted by the subsystems before reaching the OCS level.
1~Failure conditions should not cascade.
10~That is, failure of one subsystem should not affect other, working, subsystems, including communication links.
126~The THEMAS system shall control the heating and cooling units that are defined as part of the THEMAS system.
88~The definitions of the individual heating and cooling systems reside in an initialization file.
12~The system shall read this file and the definitions shall be derived from the initialization data in the file.
3044~The THEMAS system shall use the information contained in the initialization data file to determine which heating and cooling units are part of the THEMAS system.
2970~There is one heating and cooling unit that corresponds to one thermostat in each of four quadrants on each of three floors of the office building.
11~Each thermostat shall have a unique identifier by which that thermostat is identified in the THEMAS system.
2~This procedure will load these definitions into the THEMAS software.
160~Each quadrant of each floor shall have a thermostat which is to be used to provide temperature data to the THEMAS system.
7~The initialization file shall contain a unique identifier for each thermostat that the system is to monitor.
2~These identifiers shall be read from the initialization file and loaded into the THEMAS system during the initialization process.
3412~There shall be a maximum number of heating or cooling units that can be on at any given time.
10~This procedure loads the maximum number of concurrently running units allowed.
1762~The maximum number of heating or cooling units that can run concurrently shall reside in an initialization file.
4~The maximum number of concurrently running units shall be read from the initialization file and stored in the THEMAS system.
12~The trigger value is used in combination with the current temperature to determine when a heating or cooling unit shall be turned on or off.
1~The trigger values shall reside in an initialization file.
26~This procedure shall read the initialization file and establish the trigger value from the data in that file.
4~The THEMAS system shall ensure the temperature reported by a given thermostat shall not exceed a maximum deviation value of 3 degrees Fahrenheit.
1~The overtemperature values shall reside in an initialization file.
26~This procedure shall read the initialization file and establish the overtemperature value from the data in that file.
4~The THEMAS system shall only respond to temperatures that are within a reasonable value.
1~The valid temperature range value shall reside in an initialization file.
26~This procedure shall read the initialization file and establish the valid temperature range from the data in it.
94~When the THEMAS system is initialized, it shall first turn off all the heating and cooling units.
18~Then, it shall check all the thermostats and determine if any thermostat's settings require a heating or cooling unit to be turned on back on.
2048~This process shall first determine a known state of all the heating and cooling units by issuing a request to turn off all the units.
134~It shall then read the current temperature values and current temperature settings of each thermostat.
5502~If the settings reflect a need for a heating or cooling unit to be turned o n, the process shall issue a request to turn on the appropriate unit.
23~This determination shall be made in accordance with the rules outlined in section 3.1.4.1 and 3.1.4.2.
13~The THEMAS system shall only respond to temperatures from the thermostats that are within the specified valid range.
284~Two types of temperature data shall be recognized from the thermostats: 1) the temperature setting and 2) the current temperature.
4~This module shall process both types of data.
240~A current temperature value that is received from an individual thermostat shall be compared to the valid temperature range values.
5015328~If the current temperature value is strictly less than the lower value of the valid temperature range or if the received temperature value is strictly greater than the upper value of the valid temperature range, then the THEMAS system shall identify the current temperature value as an invalid temperature and shall output an invalid temperature status.
1~Otherwise, the THEMAS system shall output a valid temperature status.
328~A temperature setting value that is received from an individual thermostat shall be compared to the valid temperature range values.
166364820~If the temperature setting value is strictly less than the lower value of the valid temperature range or if the temperature setting value is strictly greater than the upper value of the valid temperature range, then the THEMAS system shall identify the temperature setting as an invalid temperature and shall output an invalid temperature status.
11~Otherwise, the THEMAS system shall realize the value for that thermostat's temperature setting.
494~The THEMAS system shall determine wh en a reported temperature or a changed temperature setting exceeds the limits set by the overtemperature values.
2~Temperatures that exceed the overtemperature limits shall be reported as such.
2~Temperatures that do not exceed these limits shall be output for subsequent processing.
3~The THEMAS system shall compare the reported temperature value to the temperature setting and detect when the temperature value exceeds the specified limits.
96320~To clarify these conditions, the following definitions will be used: LO : Lower Overtemperature Value = TSET OD UO : Upper Overtemperature Value = TSET + OD If T = LO or UO = T then the THEMAS system shall recognize this condition as the temperature limit has been exceeded.
5~In this case this process shall output the condition of the temperature limit having been exceeded.
1~If LO = T = UO, then this process shall output the temperature status.
676~When the current temperature value exceeds the current temperature setting by a predefined amount, the THEMAS system shall activate the appropriate heating or cooling units.
3060~There are two conditions for each individual thermostat that shall be tested for: 1) the thermostat's settings are satisfied and 2) the thermostat's temperature indica tes it requires a heating or cooling unit to be turned on.
1024~To clarify these conditions, the following definitions will be used: LT : Lower Trigger Value = TSET TD UT : Upper Trigger Value = TSET + TD Condition 1: LT = T = UT This condition indicates the thermostat's current temperature setting is satisfied.
48~If this condition is true, then the module shall output a request to turn off both the heating unit and the cooling unit.
318~Condition 2: LO = LT or UT T = UO This condition the need for a heating or cooling unit to be turned on.
120~If this condition is true, then this module shall output a request to turn on the heating unit if LO = T LT or the cooling unit if UT T = UO.
277~The THEMAS system shall control each of the heating and cooling units that are defined for the system.
6~The THEMAS system shall limit the number of heating or cooling units t hat may be running simultaneously.
30~The THEMAS system shall maintain the ON or OFF status of each heating and cooling unit.
60~When a request to turn on or off a heating or cooling unit, the following processing will occur.
15~When a request to turn on a heating or cooling unit is received, the system shall determine if the request can be honored.
594~If the maximum number of heating or cooling units is already running, the request will be added to a LIFO queue.
2790~If the maximum number of heating or cooling units is not running, this process will generate a request to turn on the requested unit.
570~When a request to turn off a heating or cooling unit is received, this process shall check the queue of waiting heating and cooling requests.
160~If the queue is not empty, this process shall remove one request from the LIFO queue and check the current state of the thermostat for which the queued request was made.
108~If that thermostat still needs a heating o r cooling unit turned on, this process shall submit a request to turn that unit on.
303~When a request for a heating unit or cooling to be turned is denied, an event shall be generated and the THEMAS system shall record that event.
10~The information in these events will be used for creating statistical reports.
90~This procedure shall realize the thermostat and heating or cooling information and use this information to generate a specific system event.
7574~This system event shall consist of a description of the event type (a request denied event), a designation of the thermostat that made the request, and a designation of the heating or cooling unit that was not turned on.
126~The THEMAS system shall control the heating and cooling units that are designated as part of the THEMAS system.
48~When a request to turn on or off a heating or cooling unit is made, this process shall generate the appropriate request to carry out that request.
2233~This request shall include the designation of the heating or cooling unit and a flag to indicate whether that unit is to be turned on or off.
12~Once a request to turn on or off a heating or cooling unit, the THEMAS system shall provide the necessary control signal for the unit.
3~This control signal shall also provide an indication of the unit's status at the requesting thermostat.
4780~This process shall recognize the values for the requested heating or cooling unit and the ON or OFF status that is being requested.
13~The necessary signal to the heating or cooling unit will be generated.
8~Since the interface to control the units has not been defined, the part of this process that will issue the signal is designated as TBD(to be determined).
392~In order to provide an indication of the status of the heating or cooling unit back to the requesting thermostat, this procedure shall output the status information of the heating or cooling unit.
90~In order to provide an operational history and statistical reports, this process shall generate an event each time a change is made to the status of a heating or cooling unit.
1646~This ev ent shall contain the identification of the heating or cooling unit whose status is being changed.
20~There are two events that shall result in an alarm condition: 1) an invalid temperature value is reported from a thermostat, or 2) the reported temperature has exceeded the defined limits.
4~This process shall determine which alarm event is to be generated.
24~When the THEMAS system detects a request for an alarm, this process shall detect which of the two alarms are being requested.
6~If the system detects an invalid temperature, this process shall output a continuous series of alternating 500 Hz and 700 Hz beeps on the supervisor's computer.
5~Each beep shall have a three quarter second duration.
3~This series of beeps shall continue until the supervisor manually resets the alarm through the supervisor's interface window.
4~If the system detects a temperature limit has been exceeded, this process shall output a continuous series of alternating 1000 Hz and 1500 Hz beeps on the supervisor's computer.
2~Each beep shall have a one half second duration.
3~This series of beeps shall continue until the supervisor manually resets the alarm through the supervisor's interface window.
2~Each time an alarm is requested, an alarm event shall be recorded.
5~This event shall be used to provide operational and statistical reports about the system.
10~For each event that is generated, the THEMAS system shall identify each event and generate the appropriate event data.
3~When an event occurs, the THEMAS system shall identify the event type and format an appropriate event message.
18~The THEMAS system shall record each event by a description and the current system time for that event.
6~This information shall be recorded in a Microsoft Access database that shall reside on the supervisor's computer.
41~The THEMAS system shall provide the supervisor a mechanism to change the temperature setting of any of the thermostats in the system.
4~The supervisor's interface shall display the available thermostats and their individual current temperature settings.
100~The supervisor shall be able to select one of the thermostats and select a value for the current temperature from a list of valid temperatures.
4500~The THEMAS shall provide the ability for the supervisor to select between two different types of reports: 1) the operational history of the THEMAS system for the past twelve months and 2) a statistical summary for any selected mont h.
11~The percentage of time each heating and cooling unit was on for each day of that month.
1953~For each thermometer, the ratio of the number of requests for a heating and cooling unit that was granted to the number of requests for a heating or cooling unit that was denied.

60792~In the fixed (static) mode of operation (base or data processing megacenter), the data management services shall have the capability of being tuned by on site personnel to adjust for varying workloads and sizes of associated databases.
570~These workloads and databases are expected to change more frequently and to a greater extent than for processing associated with deployed units

154952~The system will be capable of controlling the Predator and Outrider AVs with a single control system, using existing military services standard hardware and software, and supporting interfaces with various C4I systems.
23~The system shall consist of the system workstation Hardware Configuration Items (HWCIs), system Computer Software Configuration Items (CSCIs), and additional system Support HWCIs and CSCIs.
47~All system HWCI's and CSCI's shall be Year 2000 compliant.
9200~The primary function of the system is to provide command and control of the payload, Air Vehicle (AV), data link, and other necessary support equipment in order to employ tactical UAVs to conduct reconnaissance, surveillance, target acquisition, and target identification missions.
4296~The system will interface with and export and disseminate payload data to military supported units via external (not part of system) tactical communications systems, and C4I systems.
4820~Communications procedures, formats, and interfaces will be interoperable with selected standard DoD C4I systems, architectures, and protocols.
2492~The operational capabilities to be performed by the system will be determined by task analysis in accordance with MIL STD 1388 Task 401 as a guide based on a thorough understanding of Outrider and Predator mission requirements.
8~Tasks will be evaluated and allocated based on operator skills and proficiencies.
326~The initial system task analysis will produce a system baseline which will be optimized by engineering analysis and operator evaluations.
7680~The system shall meet the applicable capability and characteristic criteria established by the Operational Requirements Document (ORD) CAF 003 90 I A for the RQ 1A Predator Medium Altitude Endurance Unmanned Aerial Vehicle or System (MAE UAV) and the Close Range – Tactical Unmanned Aerial Vehicle (CR TUAV).
3~Appendix B and C identify applicable capability and characteristic criteria to system.
54~The system shall provide software capabilities and hardware configurations necessary to fulfill the operational tasking requirements across the 5 levels of interaction.
8~The states of operation of the system shall include Startup, Operation, and Shutdown.
1~The system states shall not exist concurrently.
4~Upon application of power the system shall enter the Startup State.
32~The Startup State shall be comprised of the following modes: Normal Startup Mode and Recovery Startup Mode.
44~The system will execute the particular startup mode which corresponds with the manner in which the system Software was halted.
60~When the system Software is terminated normally the system shall enter the Normal Startup Mode of operation upon application of power.
108~When the system software is halted due to an unplanned power interruption as well as abnormal program termination, then the system shall enter the Recovery Startup Mode upon application of power.
1~During startup, the system shall determine which of the 5 levels of interaction are achievable by the system configuration.
50~Levels of interaction higher than those achievable by a particular system configuration shall be prohibited.
38~The system shall inform the operator if the operator attempts to execute a function prohibited as a result of the determined level of interaction.
115928~When executing in the Normal Startup Mode, the system shall provide the system functionality necessary to initialize the system to place it in the Operations State within 60 seconds from the time power is supplied and the system application is launched.
320~Initialization of the system HWCIs shall include startup of HWCIs, download of software, startup of CSCIs, execution of Startup FD or L, and establishment of the state of readiness of all interfaces.
32~The system in the Recovery Startup Mode shall provide the system functionality to resume the Operations State within 45 seconds.
95~10 Recovery of the system HWCIs shall include startup of HWCIs, download of software, startup of CSCIs, and establishment of the state of readiness of all interfaces.
40~The system shall be capable of automatically recording system state data, interface communications and other information necessary to support event reconstruction.
926640~For recovery from abnormal termination, system shall prompt the operator to select the type of recovery to be executed: Resume in the Same Modes And Data, Resume in the Same Modes but Review and Modify the Command Data as Necessary or, Perform a Command Shutdown and Startup Via the Normal Startup Mode.
30~When in the Operations State the system shall be capable of operating in three modes: normal operations mode, training operations mode, and maintenance operations mode.
2~The Operations State modes shall not exist concurrently.
64~The operator shall have the capability to command the system to the Shutdown State from all modes under the Operations State.
7168~The system hardware and software shall execute periodic Fault Detection or Location (FD or L) while in the Normal Operations Mode and Training Mode, to include a periodic determination of level of interaction..
528~Functions under the Normal Operations Mode shall operate concurrently without precluding or excluding any of the other functions, in accordance with allowable operations as determined by the appropriate levels of interaction.

1281997~Upon the selection of a shutdown command the system shall enter the Shutdown State, which will cause the system to be placed in a condition where power can be removed without impacting operations as well as causing damage to the system, and from which restart of the system can be accomplished normally..
